As we stand on the eve of Shabbos Rosh Chodesh Shevat, I cannot refrain from mentioning the day of the Hillula of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, on the tenth of Shevat. Anyone who knows a chapter in the history of our people in the country of Russia—and especially those who emigrated from there—knows that this day evokes memories and spiritual experiences from the period of the struggle for the existence of Torah, mitzvos, and holy institutions under the banner of the Baal HaHillula. He literally risked his life to preserve the values of our people and our way of life, with actual mesiras nefesh, day after day. You are surely familiar with this illustrious chapter, at least in general terms. One of the most important points in his struggle and activities during that period, and throughout his entire life, was in the area of chinuch al taharas hakodesh, the fruits and the fruits of whose fruits are still visible there at this time, some fifty years later.

It is understood that any act of remembrance in the life of a Jew must lead to an increase in action in his own sphere, and in this case, an increase in action in accordance with the worldview and the aspirations of the Baal HaHillula, may his merit protect us.

With respect and blessing, and with regards to Rabbi Dov Ber, may he live, Levi.

## 815

B"H, 28 Teves, 5722
Brooklyn.

Mrs. Rachel, may she live.

Greetings and blessings!

Following a hiatus, I received your letter from Motzaei Shabbos, 24 Teves, outlining the activities during the time since you last wrote.

It was especially pleasant for me to read about the success of the gan, and I have a strong hope that you are also working on increasing the number of children, as surely, even in the neighborhood of the gan, more residents are arriving from among the new immigrants and the like.

You conclude your letter on a positive note regarding the shiur for women from Sephardic backgrounds. This is a matter of the greatest importance, especially since there is a unique challenge regarding these women: before their arrival in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established), the order of their lives was based on fulfilling the command of their father, and subsequently their husband, without any hesitation or thought of the possibility of not listening. Suddenly, they are transplanted into an atmosphere, etc., where they are told not to listen, etc., and that they must be independent—not only regarding their physical father and husband, but also, etc. Therefore, it is urgent to establish them in the truth: that even in their current place, the Torah and mitzvos remain in full force. On the contrary, they are now in the land upon which the eyes of Hashem your G-d are from the beginning of the year until the end of the year; they are in the King’s palace. Therefore, this independence must be expressed through an increase in Jewish values and the standards of Yiddishkeit, to the point of influencing the husband, the children, and the entire standing of the home in the aforementioned direction.

It is understood that the approach must be adapted to the level of understanding and knowledge of the various circles of these women. However, it seems that the content is essential for all circles—both the most observant and those who are, etc. Fortunate is the portion of anyone who acts in this regard, and we have been promised that one who comes to purify himself is assisted.

With blessings for good tidings in the aforementioned, and also in your personal matters.

The Pidyon Nefesh for 10 Shevat was received.

## 8,816

B"H, 28 Teves, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 24 Teves, in which you write about the difficulties in Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in... and what the administration told you, and that you did not want to comply.

It is understandable that this is not the right way to act. As has been explained many times in our Torah, Toras Chaim, a student of a yeshiva—and especially of a Chassidishe yeshiva—must follow the Rosh Yeshiva and the administration, even if it seems otherwise to him, and even if he does not fully understand their directive.

And this is no wonder, for the yetzer hara will not come to a Yid and tell him straight away at the beginning, "Do the opposite of the Shulchan Aruch," because one would not listen to him. But when he suggests that the student understands more than the Rosh Yeshiva, he can thus lure the student into following him—the "old and foolish king."

I hope it is unnecessary to elaborate on this. When you receive this letter, you will change your stance and take upon yourself to carry out all the instructions of the administration. Most importantly, you must learn with hasmadah and shekidah according to the schedules of the yeshiva and the class to which you will be assigned. As mentioned, through learning with hasmadah and shekidah, you will ascend from class to class, as per the promise of our Sages, of blessed memory: "You have labored and you have found."

Surely you are keeping up with the three daily shiurim of Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya, as is well known.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

Understandably, you may show this letter to the yeshiva administration; a separate letter to them is not necessary.

**Promise:** Megillah 6b.

## 8,517

B"H, 29 Teves, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 25 Teves, in which you write about your state of mind and general situation.

Regarding such matters, the advised counsel is to keep engraved in your mind the concepts of *Hashgacha Pratis*, which is one of the foundations of our faith and our holy Torah—the *Torah Chaim*. This is especially true according to what is explained at length in the teachings of Chassidus Chabad: how *Hashgacha Pratis* applies to every single person, down to the most minute details. Since the *Ribono shel Olam* is the essence of good, and it is the nature of the Good to bestow good, this inherently negates the possibility of worry and sadness. As the sweet singer of Israel says, "Hashem is with me, I will not fear," and "Hashem is my shepherd, I shall not lack." This is particularly relevant given that you have seen and continue to see the kindnesses of the *Ribono shel Olam* in practice, having been blessed with a husband and lovely children, etc. Furthermore, you have merited and succeeded in having a share and participating in *chinuch* on *taharas hakodesh*, bringing the hearts of *Bnei Yisrael* closer to our Father in Heaven. It is a well-known saying of the *Baal Shem Tov* that the love of the *Ribono shel Olam* for every son and daughter of *Yisrael* is incomparably greater than the love of elderly parents for an only child born to them in their old age.

Since a person must act within the natural order as it depends on them, and there is room to say that one's state of mind is also connected to the state of one's physical health—in general, it is appropriate that you consult a doctor regarding this and follow their instructions.

With blessings for success in your holy work mentioned above, as well as in the management of your home—which is also holy work and depends primarily on the woman, the *akeres habayis*—and for good news, with joy and a glad heart.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

**Secretary**

## 820

B"H, 2 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings,

In response to your letter of 24 Teves, in which you write about the good resolution you made upon receiving my previous letter, and that after some time, you were unable to withstand the test, etc.

The words of our Sages, of blessed memory, are well-known regarding such a situation: that a person’s heart should not fall within him if he did not withstand the test and failed, Heaven forbid. Certainly, he should not come to despair, Heaven forbid, in his battle with the Yetzer Hara. On the contrary, despair and a fallen spirit are among the weapons and tactics of the Yetzer Hara. He says to the person—and Israel, His close ones, are called "Adam"—that each of them is called a son of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov—to discourage the battle against him, claiming that it will not succeed, Heaven forbid, etc. On the contrary, if one has failed and sees how evil and bitter the failure is—not only that it is the opposite of the will of the Creator who gives the Torah and commands the mitzvos, but that it also brings destruction and ruin to his physical life, his life in this world—through this, a new and powerful strength will be added to him in the aforementioned battle. Ultimately, the promise is fulfilled: "A person sanctifies himself a little from below, and they sanctify him much from above."

1) In order to strengthen the aforementioned battle and hasten the victory, it is most appropriate that you endeavor not to have money on your person, except for the **most** necessary amounts to purchase **small** items and the like.

2) Associate as much as possible only with people who are observant of Torah and mitzvos. In particular, every Jew is commanded regarding this, as our Sages, of blessed memory, said: "Associate with many," and "Woe to the wicked and woe to his neighbor; good to the righteous and good to his neighbor."

3) Since many doctors know remedies to strengthen and overcome the desire that leans toward strong drinks and the like, and in time to nullify it completely, you should consult them and follow their instructions. Furthermore—and this is essential—you should choose certain chapters of Mishnayos or certain chapters of Tehillim and review them from time to time, especially at a time when it seems to you as if the desire has been aroused, contrary to the aforementioned command.

Regarding your writing that you lack the necessary consistency—among the advised strategies for this is that when you see that your consistency has diminished, you should switch your study from Halacha to Aggadah, from Torah Shebichtav to Torah Shebaal Peh, or vice versa.

Upon your receipt of this letter, the time will be close to the Yahrtzeit of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, Nasi Yisroel. Just as with every matter, in this too, each of us—and you are included, of course—must learn a lesson in one's avodah to his Creator. It is this: If in a place of literal life-threatening danger, where there is no desire in a person stronger than the desire for life, the Baal HaHillula sacrificed his soul for the sake of the Torah and mitzvos to fulfill the will of the Creator and said that this is expected of everyone—how much more so when there is no danger at all, Heaven forbid, but only a passing craving and a fleeting desire, which after a few moments one will regret, and which brings only the ruin of his life. It is simple that one must fulfill the mitzvah of the Creator and conduct oneself in the ways of pleasantness, in the ways of peace, the way of our Torah, the Torah of life.

I will await your response regarding all that is mentioned above as soon as possible, and I thank you in advance.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita

***

**The words of our Sages:** See Tanya, Chapter 28.
**The promise:** Yoma 39a.
**As our Sages said:** See Sotah 7a; Sukkah at the end.

## 822

822

B”H, 3 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

**Memorandum**

I take the liberty of bringing to your attention several points that are currently on the agenda. While these matters have surely not escaped your notice, since I have not heard that any action has been taken regarding them, I feel it is my duty to bring them to your attention due to their great importance:

(1) In recent months, as is known, thousands of families of new immigrants have arrived in our Holy Land—may it be built and established by Moshiach Tzidkeinu speedily in our days. Even though it is possible that among them are those who arrived from countries where not all Jews, without exception, were observant of Torah and mitzvos, there is certainly a significant number of Torah-observant Jews among the new arrivals.

— Furthermore, and most importantly, from the perspective of our Torah, the Torah of life, regardless of how a Jew may have conducted himself in the past, the Torah and its mitzvos are given to him anew each and every day, and he is obligated to accept them (though if his previous conduct was not as it should be, he has an additional special mitzvah: the mitzvah of teshuvah in practice). —

Now, when new immigrants of this type arrive, they are most often settled in new *moshavim* (settlements) established for them. This brings them, for a certain period of time, into contact and interaction with representatives of the offices responsible for settlement, absorption, and so on. As is human nature, the first impression is the strongest, especially when it is connected with assistance in settlement and guidance in agricultural or industrial professions, and the like.

According to the information I have received until now, to my great regret, in almost all cases, those who greet the immigrants at the port or airport, as well as the instructors, supervisors, and so on, are for the most part not observant of Torah and mitzvos. This is clearly evident immediately upon the first meeting by their conduct, even if they do not intend to emphasize their non-religiosity—and certainly when there are among them those who do wish to emphasize this, and all the more so when there are those among them who wish to exploit the situation to distance the immigrants from Torah and mitzvos. Naturally, once the immigrants fall under this influence, it is difficult afterward to find ways to return them to the proper path and bring them closer to Hashem and His Torah, for the rule is known: "Once he has transgressed and repeated it, it becomes for him as if it were permitted"—and this is even if he has only transgressed and repeated it once; all the more so if he has transgressed and repeated it several times.

Everything stated above holds true even if we were speaking of only one Jewish soul; all the more so when we are speaking of thousands of families, and it appears that thousands more new immigrant families are expected to arrive in the near future.

Therefore, it is a time to act, to take urgent and necessary measures to rectify the situation to the greatest extent possible.

In truth, there should be religious representatives at the gathering points abroad and on the ships. But even if that is not possible, it is my opinion that it is an absolute necessity that there be religious representatives—at the very least to meet the immigrants at the airport or port, and also to be with them for a period of time in their place of settlement. And regarding the immigrants who arrived in recent months and have already fallen into the aforementioned situation, there are certainly possibilities to rectify the situation to a significant degree, at the very least, if it cannot be fully rectified.

Of course, it has not escaped me that the main question will be regarding manpower—finding suitable people to meet the immigrants and stand by their side, etc. It is possible that the question of funding will also arise. But there is hope that if they begin to act on this in practice, both things will be found to a greater extent than was initially estimated. And surely, one must act even on the basis of a doubt, and all the more so when there is no doubt that much can be accomplished.

It is also understood that I have not come to suggest specific proposals on how to rectify the situation—whether through an inquiry in the Knesset or through other official channels. On one hand, one must fear that in this way, tempers will be flared, but on the other hand, in this way it is also easier to mobilize public opinion, volunteers, and so on. Furthermore, even if it is not done officially, the matter will not remain a secret, because those who wish to stir up tempers will jump at the chance to do so, at which point the hand of the *chareidim* will be at a disadvantage. In such matters, in the style of our Sages, "the judge has only what his eyes see," in its place and at its time.

It should be noted that since every matter has its advantages and disadvantages, regarding the current *aliyah*, precisely because not all the immigrants are, according to reports, observant of Torah and mitzvos, the necessity of meeting them as mentioned above is even greater. For if the immigrants were all Torah-observant, one could expect that a demand for their religious needs would come from their side, whereas if the immigrants are as described, if one does not begin [...] from the very first moment of their arrival, the *hisorerus* will not come from their side.

(2) The aforementioned question is particularly acute because a portion of the immigrants is settling in new regions. For even when a *moshav* is located in proximity to a religious *kibbutz* or a large city, it is still necessary to maintain close contact with the new *moshav*, at least at the beginning of their settlement period. And this is all the more so when the immigrants settle in a new region, and the whole concept of these new regions is that every point should be independent, without connection to *kibbutzim* and cities, but rather that everything is within it. And those who are at the head of the development of the region are, for understandable reasons, from the ruling party. Under these conditions, the new immigrants are completely cut off, and all their interaction is with representatives, agents, instructors, etc., who are not observant of Torah and mitzvos. The results are understood, and elaboration is unnecessary.

Our Sages said, "Everything depends on *mazal*." Regarding the first hundreds of children, they went out in protest and storm, and to a certain extent—there is no doubt that this had tangible results. And now, regarding thousands of families, not even an echo is heard, and apparently, there is also a lack of action.

(3) Continuing from the end of the previous section, regarding the question of absorbing youth within the framework of *Aliyat HaNoar* (Youth Aliyah), and even children at an age younger than that, the problem is not only to act so that the children are handed over for religious education, but that there must also be sufficient absorption places. And the question of absorption places is not so much a question of expenses for food, drink, lodging, and the ongoing expenses of education, because in any event, the existing absorption places are approaching their peak capacity and are running out of space; that is to say, the main problem is new buildings, dormitories, and new absorption centers. And this is connected to sums of money that are much larger in proportion than those for supply needs.

On the other hand, in the government budget and in the budgets of the various ministries, and also for the Jewish Agency, there are significant sums allocated for housing and absorption, as the necessity to house the public exists in any case.

From this, it follows that now is the opportune time to act on a systematic and fundamental point: that from the housing budgets, certain sums should be allocated for youth dormitories for the age of *Aliyat HaNoar*, and also for dormitories for children of a younger age—children who, for various reasons, cannot live with their parents, whether due to family conditions, or due to the fact that the parents have not yet arrived in the country, or due to the size of the families, etc.

The argument that a dormitory falls under the category of housing is justified in any case, for what difference does it make if it is housing for an entire family or housing for a part of the family members? On the contrary.

In my opinion, it is necessary to raise this question and demand as soon as possible, because after the housing budget is divided among the various ministries, etc., it will then be necessary to enter into negotiations with the directors of these ministries. This is not the case before the allocation, when there will only be general opposition. And it is likely that there will be opposition, for the other side will certainly "snake" (sense) the intention behind this demand.

## 8224

B"H, 7 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To Anash shlita who are in...
May Hashem keep them and grant them life,

Peace and blessing!

It has been a very, very long time since I have received any news regarding your activities in disseminating the wellsprings, or regarding your activities in disseminating matters of Yiddishkeit in general.

The explanation for this may be simple: that there are no activities. Or, the explanation may be in accordance with the directive of our Sages and as emphasized in the teachings of Chassidus: that there are indeed many such activities, but they are simply not being reported.

The common denominator in both scenarios is that there is no place for me to write from here to offer encouragement, and certainly not to make demands regarding the aforementioned.

Furthermore, there is also a concern involved—perhaps more than a concern—and according to the directive of our Sages, just as it is a mitzvah to say something that will be heeded, so too it is a **mitzvah** not to say something that will not be heeded.

I would also point to the Gemara in Maseches Chullin (94a): "One should not press... and he knows that he will not listen..."

This is the reason for the cessation of my writing and encouragement for activities, and there is no room for any other interpretation.

The main purpose of this notification is to absolutely rule out the possibility of interpreting this silence as an indication that I agree with a policy of "I have saved my own soul," or in the common parlance of Anash, "a tzaddik in a fur coat." Rather, it is solely because, apparently, I am unable to protest, or (according to the second scenario mentioned above, as we are obligated to judge others favorably) that there are indeed many such activities.

May it be the will of Hashem, as we stand a few days before the yahrtzeit of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, Nasi Yisroel—the tenth of Shevat—that each and every one of you will learn from the mesiras nefesh of the Baal HaHillula, mesiras nefesh for activities with others [for regarding himself, as per the words of the Arizal and as explained in the words of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe (author of the Tanya—the arbiter of the hidden Torah—and the Shulchan Aruch—the arbiter of the revealed Torah)—the soul itself requires no tikkun at all, and it only descended to draw down light to fix others, etc.],

that you will do everything in your power, and even more than that, through activities that align with the will and hopes of the Baal HaHillula. And we have been promised: "He who comes to purify himself is assisted."

With respect and blessing,

**M. Schneerson**

It is understood that the above contains absolutely no element of resentment, chas v'shalom, or anything of the sort. Rather (if the first scenario, chas v'shalom, is correct), it is pain over the lack of any action in the aforementioned areas, at a time when the King of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, took the precious stone upon which the importance of the crown of royalty depends, from the crown, and ground it, etc. A word to the wise is sufficient. Consequently, there is no place at all to write here with justifications, explanations, etc. This is a matter between man and Hashem.

***

**Directive of our Sages:** Yevamos 65b.
**As explained:** Tanya, Chapter 37 (48b).
**And we have been promised:** Yoma 38b.
**Precious stone:** See also above, Letter 7290, and the references in the footnotes there.

## 8260

The treasure of the King of the Kings of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, which is beyond any end—the level of "no eye has seen." And for the sake of winning the war—

Above, the war of *kedushah* against the *klipah*, and below, the war of the *nefesh elokis* against the *nefesh habamis*,

To strike the firstborn of Egypt—the strength and power of the *mitzrayim* (limitations) and boundaries, and the strength and power of the *klipah*—

## 8227

[11 Shevat 5722]

...Regarding your question (concerning the *sichos* and lectures on *Chabad* topics) as to whether you should concentrate your efforts and persist in one location, or visit a different place each time—

In my opinion, although the main thing is to kindle the lights (the "candle of Hashem is the soul of man") **within each and every one** of the Children of Israel, and in the manner of *beha'alosecha* (see *Likutei Torah*, beginning of *Parshas Beha'alosecha*)—

And therefore, one should increase the number of places one visits—especially since, after the initial kindling, in many instances and cases "the flame rises on its own"—

Nevertheless, it is worthwhile to also visit two or three locations in a structured, consistent manner.

Regarding your complaint that you often do not see results (on the spot)—see *Likutei Torah* regarding the distinction between sowing and planting.

## 8268

B"H, 11 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the esteemed Chassid, G-d-fearing, and dedicated to communal needs, etc.,
R' Yitzchak, may he live,

Greetings and blessings!

...In response to your questions from some time ago:

a) In *Likutei Torah*, in the explanation of *V'lo tashbit* [in the section on Parshas Behar], in the fourth point: It states that there are matters in the Torah that were not intended to be "clothed" below in wisdom and intellect at all, such as "And Yosef was brought down to Mitzrayim, and Potifar bought him..." — Why was this specific example chosen?

- It can be said that this is the very same concept: that Potifar "bought" (a purchase that the Torah testifies to its validity) Yosef, and yet Yosef was not "clothed" there at all, nor were the matters of Mitzrayim able to confuse him in the slightest (see *Torah Or*, end of Parshas Vayechi).

b) In *Torah Or*, in the *maamar* beginning *Chayav Inish* (the second one, 98d): It states that it was through [Hashem] forcing the mountain over them like a barrel that they arrived at the precedence of *Naaseh* to *Nishma*. However, in *Midrash Tanchuma* and in *Tosafos* (*Shabbos* 88a), it is evident that the precedence of *Naaseh* to *Nishma* occurred *before* the forcing of the mountain.

The Midrashim are divided on this. As it is written in *Torah Or*, so too is it found in *Midrash Tehillim* 1:4, and in *Mechilta d'Rashbi*.

## 8229

B"H, 11 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Blessings and peace!

In response to your letter of 2 Shevat, in which you outline the main points of your life history and studies, and conclude that the manner of your studies—and especially the content of your education and upbringing until now—has caused a sense of emptiness and a decline from time to time, etc.

Now, a situation such as this is not such a rare phenomenon, especially among the youth in these most recent years, and for several reasons. Yet, as in all times and places, the verse in our Torah, the Torah of life, remains in full force today: "See, I have set before you today life and the good... therefore choose life" (Devarim 30:15-19). That is to say:

No matter the situation in which a person finds themselves, the choice is always given to choose life and the good. And in the phrasing of our Sages, of blessed memory: "He who comes to purify himself is assisted." 

For ultimately, the inwardness of the soul and the essence of the soul is good. When a person begins an action to improve, even the external layers of the soul receive assistance from within—and sometimes, assistance of a magnitude that was not anticipated in advance. However, the beginning of this process must be in a manner that is most influential upon the person; namely, a fixed and consistent conduct in actual practice, which aligns with the worldview to which one aspires and according to which one wishes to organize one's life.

In other words, conduct in daily life that conforms to the instructions of our Torah, the Torah of life, and the fulfillment of its mitzvos, regarding which it is said, "and he shall live by them."

It has already been explained at length in several places that the fact that our Torah is called the "Torah of life" has a twofold meaning:
1) How to conduct oneself in life.
2) How to conduct oneself in a manner that life is truly worthy of the name "life," and not merely a framework of days, weeks, months, and years that are emptied of all content.

And since, as is implied by the tone of your letter, you are at an age where the energy granted to a person is still in its fullness, and you are in those years when a person’s character and the manner of their life—as they will build their home—are being formed and established, it means that you have the full ability, as mentioned above, to fulfill the good advice and the commandment: "therefore choose life."

May it be Hashem's will that you bring good news regarding all that has been mentioned, including your influence upon your surroundings to instill this aforementioned worldview.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

***

 **And in the phrasing of our Sages:** Yoma 38b.

## 830

B"H, 13 Shevat, 5722

As is the custom of Yisrael, to seek out and find in everything a connection to the weekly parsha, so too regarding the content of your letter and its enclosure—this response will (if the translation is ready) hopefully reach you at the beginning of the coming week, Parshas Yisro, the parsha of Matan Torah. For there, a clear answer and indication is given to the question: "Who is a Jew?"

In the words of the pasuk: "And now, if you will indeed hearken to My voice and keep My covenant, you shall be to Me a treasure among all the peoples... and you shall be to Me a kingdom of priests and a holy nation."

May it be the will of Hashem that just as at the time of Matan Torah, as the pasuk states, "And Yisrael encamped there opposite the mountain," and as our Sages, of blessed memory, note—*vayichan* (encamped) is written in the singular form, indicating that all Jews united to stand beside that famous mountain—that this should enter forever into the heart of every Jew, and also into the consciousness of all nations, that this is the Mountain of Hashem, upon which the Torah was given, an eternal Torah for all times and for all lands.

May this now penetrate not only into the hearts, but also into the thoughts of all Jews of this generation, and from the head (thought) may it descend into actual practice, to fulfill the mitzvos of Hashem Yisbarach in deed, mitzvos regarding which the holy Torah says, "And he shall live by them."

With blessings for a good Shabbos and with blessings for good tidings of good health.

## 831

B"H, Erev Shabbos Kodesh, 15 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the Rabbinic scholar, the G-d-fearing, the distinguished, the man of many accomplishments and fine character, etc., Levi, may he live.

Shalom u’Bracha!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter containing the invitation and notification regarding the *siyum* of the *rabim* on *Maseches Kiddushin*, and the *siyum* celebration on Motzaei Shabbos, the eve of the day of *Matan Torah*.

A word spoken in its time is good, for the *siyum* of the *Maseches*—*Rabbi Nehorai says, etc.*—speaks of the greatness of Torah, a greatness that pertains not only to the World to Come and its matters, but also to this world and its matters. Indeed, the emphasis is on how it is better than any craft in the world regarding the reward in this world.

It is worth noting regarding this *siyum* that, seemingly, we find here the opposite of the known rule that a *Baraisa* adds to the matters in the *Mishnah*, explaining and detailing them more. Since "the decrees multiplied and hearts diminished," the "brevity of words" of the *Mishnah*—which is a "concise matter encompassing many subjects"—is insufficient due to the decline of the generations. Yet, in the *Baraisa* of Rabbi Nehorai, it is the opposite: the words of the *Mishnah* are more detailed, as it mentions sickness, old age, and suffering, and not merely old age as in the *Baraisa*. Likewise, the *Mishnah* contains the reason for the importance of Torah—that its reward is in this world and the World to Come, whereas all other crafts are only in this world—a reason omitted in the *Baraisa*.

— Incidentally: The citation (in the margin) for this *Baraisa* as "Maseches Sofrim, Chapter 16" belongs to the statement in the *Mishnah*, for in *Maseches Sofrim* (and similarly in the *Tosefta*, end of Chapter 5), it is in the same language as the *Mishnah*.

This can be understood—briefly—with the premise that the subject matter, both in the *Mishnah* and the *Baraisa*, concerns teaching one’s son. According to the principle, "Train a lad according to his way," the teacher must encourage him in his studies with things that are dear to him at his young age, saying to him, "Read, etc.," but not for the sake of the reading itself, as he does not yet know its greatness. A child at the age of education (as well as women and the general populace) does not yet belong to the level of learning Torah *lishmah*, to be like servants who serve the Master not for the sake of receiving a reward, "until their knowledge increases and they become wise with extra wisdom."

There is also a distinction in the level of the son: in the generation of the *Mishnah*, the early ones were like angels, and the son was also attached to and yearned for the reward of the World to Come; therefore, through this, it was possible to draw him to Torah study. This is not the case in the generations that followed, where we are like donkeys, not yearning for the World to Come; therefore, the reward of the World to Come was not mentioned in the *Baraisa*.

The same applies to the distinction regarding the reward of a craft versus Torah in this world: in the later generations, where "every man’s way is right in his own eyes," and "love (self-love) covers all transgressions," one does not fear sickness and suffering, for there is no suffering without sin, and he believes in himself that he will not stumble in this. Regarding sickness, he is confident in himself that "if he conducts himself in these ways that we have instructed, I guarantee him that he will not come to sickness all his days."

This is not so in the early generations, the generation of the *Mishnah*, who feared even sickness and suffering—following the words of the *Mishnah*, "Do not believe in yourself until the day of your death." Likewise regarding sickness, he does not believe in himself, fearing he might stumble in "bad habits" and become ill—and therefore, it is specifically in the *Mishnah* that sickness and suffering are mentioned.

* * *

Regarding the aforementioned matter and its explanation, one should connect—as is the custom of Israel—the *siyum* of the *Maseches* with its beginning: "A woman is acquired, etc., with money, a document, and intimacy."

For the study of Torah, which must be in a manner as if it were given from Sinai today, the day of *Matan Torah* (to the point that we are commanded: "Just as there [at the time of *Matan Torah*] it was with awe and fear, so too here it must be with awe and fear"), is the concept of the woman (the clarified one—as per the precision of *Tosafos*—this is *Knesses Yisrael*) being acquired by her husband (this is *Hakadosh Baruch Hu*). As our Sages of blessed memory said, "On the day of his wedding—this is *Matan Torah*."

And in this, there are three ways—as mentioned above regarding the order of study and how "the teacher should encourage":

With money—material reward.

With a document—which is not worth a *prutah*, but he writes to her, "You are betrothed to me"—he sanctifies her to himself and forbids her to the entire world like a *hekdesh*, and "gathers her shame"—spiritual reward.

However, there is no complete union with the husband, because "he does not act for His sake, but only to receive a reward."

And with intimacy—(and in the language of the writer) "and he shall cleave to his wife and they shall become one flesh."

This is the perfection of study—not for the sake of receiving a reward at all—where then *Yisrael*, *Orayta*, and *Kudsha Brich Hu* are all one.

With respect and blessing,

## 832

16 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Rabbi [Name],
Shalom u’vracha,

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter, which arrived on Shabbos, Tu B’Shvat.

It is understood and self-evident that, on the contrary, it gave me pleasure to learn that you are studying and also engaging in *chochmas ha’emes*.

As is well known, the explanation of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, regarding the precise language of the Gemara in *Berachos* 63b, "and they engaged in Torah," is that the study of Torah must be like a business (*esek*)—where one makes an effort to find customers for it, to publicize it, and to exert oneself with as much labor as possible, even to the point of disseminating it "outside," etc.

The comment I made in my previous letter regarding this was, on the contrary, to suggest an addition to the above: by explaining the concepts brought in the books of Kabbalah, which is absolutely necessary according to the command and warning of the Baal Shem Tov, as cited in *Shoresh Mitzvas HaTefillah*, end of Chapter 2, which I noted in my previous letter.

Regarding your comment in your letter—based on the reason for the Baal Shem Tov’s warning to strip away physicality, etc., as explained in his teachings—it is also understood that a brief explanation is insufficient, just as it is insufficient in the books you mention in your letter. Furthermore, Torah is one, and it is analogous to the study of *nigleh*: one would not be satisfied with the words of the *Mishnah* without studying the *Gemara*, nor with the *Rambam* without studying his commentators. Certainly, for someone of your stature, there is no need for me to elaborate further on this.

As for your writing that "not every mind can bear this," after asking your forgiveness, this does not seem to be the case, given the many places and the multitude of warnings to disseminate *pnimiyus haTorah*, going from strength to strength and increasing in light, specifically in these generations of ours. And Hashem does not deal tyrannically with His creatures, and *tzaddikim* are like their Creator. Since they demand, repeat, and emphasize the necessity of the aforementioned study, the ability has certainly been granted to everyone who is called by the name of Israel to **engage** in this study. And I mean not only for oneself, but as mentioned above, in the manner of a business. And if you wish to say so, this too is learned from the words of the Sages in their *Mishnah*: "Who is wise? He who learns from every person."

To note from the teachings of the Baal Shem Tov on this *Mishnah*, it is transcribed in the *Sefer HaZichronos* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, page 344.

Regarding what you wrote at the end of your letter concerning the *Rambam* and the wisdom of Kabbalah, which he did not study:

It is so in *Shaar HaGilgulim*, Introduction 36, and in the commentary of Maharash Alkabetz on *Shir HaShirim*. See also the Chida’s *Shem HaGedolim*, entry "Rambam." It has always been difficult for me to reconcile this with what I heard from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that his father [the Rebbe Maharash] said that he had a tradition, teacher from teacher, up to our Master the Baal Shem Tov, that the *Rambam* was a great *mekubal*. The reason he was careful not to reveal this even by way of allusion (as Rashi’s commentary suggests) was because it was a dangerous time to reveal matters of Kabbalah, even by way of allusion.

Perhaps the matter can be resolved by saying that he reached this at the end of his days, and in the mode of *ibur* (and in this, it differs from the Ramban). Indeed, it is brought in many places that at the end of his days, the *Rambam* engaged in Kabbalah; *Migdal Oz*, *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* ch. 1 and 2; *Responsa Maharam Alshakar*, siman 117; *Avodas HaKodesh*, part 2, ch. 13; *Levush*, *Levush Panim Yekarot*, part 1, ch. 53; and see *Shomer Emunim*, *Vikuach* 1, subsection 13; the commentary of R. M. Butril to *Sefer Yetzirah*, ch. 4, mishna 3.

With respect and blessing to increase in the aforementioned engagement, with proper health, joy, and a good heart.

## 833

B”H, 16 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Blessings and peace!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated January 16th. You ask what you should believe: the path in which you were educated for years, or what you heard from a certain individual over the course of two evenings.

Surely you have heard the expression "return to the sources" several times. You certainly also know that education does not operate in a vacuum. Every person, even before their formal education begins, possesses certain matters rooted in their soul—matters whose source is the inner dimension of the soul. Furthermore, education lacks the power to change these matters; it can only cover them for a longer or shorter period of time. There are many proofs for this in the science of education, in medicine, in biology, and so on.

Therefore, we often see that a single occurrence or a brief conversation—which is not "education" at all—can fundamentally change a person. The reason is that the person only needed a catalyst to remove the thing that was covering what was already present in the inner reaches of their soul.

What I have written above is the answer to your question regarding what you should believe. The very fact that you were so deeply impressed by what you heard over the course of just two evenings is proof of what our Sages of blessed memory have taught us for thousands of years: **that all Yidden** are believers, the children of believers. It is just that, at times, this faith is covered by a layer of foreign and external matters. Fortunate is the portion of anyone for whom this layer is removed early on, allowing them to stand upon the truth and return to their source.

It is my hope that these lines, though few in quantity relative to the importance of the matter, will suffice to shed light on the subject. I look forward to hearing good news regarding this.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

**P.S.** At a time of favor, please mention your mother, shetichyeh, at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for whatever she may need.

***

 **That our Sages have taught us:** Shabbos 97a.

## 8334

B"H, 16 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed and distinguished individual, occupied with communal needs, Rabbi Pinchas Aharon, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

After a long interval, I received your letter from Wednesday, in which you write regarding the refusal to allow certain details to be engraved on a tombstone concerning the deceased, and that for this reason, the son, may he live, wishes to exhume the deceased and move them to another cemetery.

Generally, in such matters, one must turn to a Rav who is a *posek* for halachic questions, and surely you will do so.

However, due to the urgency of the matter—as it is well known how much our Sages of blessed memory emphasized the honor of the dead, **not** to exhume them, etc.—I am writing this letter as well, with the certainty that he will reconsider the aforementioned thought, and they will rest in peace.

It would be appropriate to explain that we are commanded regarding the honor of one’s father and mother, to honor them in their lifetime and in their death.

The essential point in this is conduct in daily life that conforms to the instruction of our Torah, the Torah of life, and the fulfillment of its *mitzvos*, regarding which it is said, "and he shall live by them." This is the greatest *nachas ruach* for the soul in the World of Truth, where our Torah, the Torah of truth, and its matters are of the greatest value, and *kal v'chomer* (it is understood).

With blessings for good news.

**P.S.** Surely, even though you do not mention it, you gathered on the day of the *hilula* of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *Nasi Yisroel*, the tenth of Shevat, with the purpose of every *farbrengen* being to take from it for the days, weeks, and months ahead, in accordance with the hopes and will of the *baal hilula*.

#### 8334

**Rabbi Pinchas Aharon, may he live:** Weberman, Miami. Additional letters to him appear above in 4191 and in the references in the notes there.

**Not to exhume:** See also above, Vol. 13, letter 4485, 4524. Below, 8592.

**We are commanded:** *Kiddushin* 31b.

## 8. Letter 8

B”H, Erev Shabbos Kodesh, Parshas Yisro, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the participants in the annual dinner of Merkaz HaYeshivos Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch,
May they live,

Peace and blessing!

I hope that all those who have come to unite with Merkaz HaYeshivos Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch on the occasion of this annual event have brought with them the spirit of the parsha—the parsha of **Aseres HaDibros** and **Matan Torah**, which we read on Shabbos, one day prior to the event.

* * *

The Aseres HaDibros unite within themselves two sets of fundamental laws that, at first glance, appear highly disparate: the first commandments, **Anochi, Lo Yihyeh Lecha**, and so on, express and reveal the deepest concepts of Achdus Hashem; the others, however, contain such simple commandments as **Lo Tirtzach** and **Lo Tignov**, which are, in essence, self-evident even to the average human intellect.

But the truth is that if one were to leave moral laws—even the "self-evident" ones—to human intellect alone, without their Divine foundation and power, human intellect, driven by self-love, could twist them to the point where a transgression is interpreted as a "mitzvah." By interpreting the commandments of "Lo Tirtzach" and "Lo Tignov" from the standpoint of self-interest, many nations of the world and many individuals have "koshered" their own abominable goals, not to mention justifying the means used to achieve those goals—as has, to our regret, been clearly demonstrated, especially in these very recent years.

If, by rejecting or even separating the "Anochi" and "Lo Yihyeh Lecha" from "Lo Tirtzach" and "Lo Tignov," humanity could not be protected from murder and theft in their most brutal forms, it is certainly the case that one cannot be protected from "Lo Tirtzach" and "Lo Tignov" in more "refined" ways, such as *shfichus damim* through *malbin pnei chaveiro* (shaming one’s fellow), or *geneivas da’as*, and the like.

The Aseres HaDibros emphasize—and experience has confirmed this repeatedly—that even the simplest laws of morality and ethics must have the Divine foundation of **Anochi** and **Lo Yihyeh Lecha**; only then do they have true existence.

The aforementioned is also the primary purpose and task of *chinuch al taharas hakodesh*: to implant in our children their **derech hachayim** according to the **Toras Chayim**—a way of life, in daily living, upon the firm ground of Torah and mitzvos, which provide the true content of life and are simultaneously the source of life for every Jew and for all Jews.

* * *

The founders of Merkaz HaYeshivos Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch—our Rebbes, the Nesi’im, and specifically here in America, the Rebbe, my father-in-law, of blessed memory—established the Lubavitcher yeshivos so that they would always, everywhere, and at all times embody this foundation with a deeper understanding and deeper feeling, as is illuminated and explained in Toras HaChassidus. This has endowed the Lubavitcher yeshivos and the Lubavitcher talmidim with a special uniqueness and special merits.

It is therefore a special *zechus* to have a personal share in the Lubavitcher yeshivos—by helping them not only to keep their doors open and maintain their current state, but also to enable their expansion and branching out in accordance with the serious demands and the necessity of the hour.

May Hashem Yisbarach bless all the friends and supporters of Merkaz HaYeshivos Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch, them and their families, *shlita*—in all that they need, both materially and spiritually.

With respect and blessing,

**Menachem Schneerson**

## 839

B.H., 25 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished, etc., Moreinu Harav Elazar, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter, as well as your book *She'ar Yashuv* on the Torah, which I have received.

May it be the will of Hashem that for many more years you will continue to add strength to the study of Torah—both the revealed aspects and the inner dimensions which, in our generation, have been revealed in the teachings of Chassidus, the teachings of the Baal Shem Tov, his students, and his students' students. This is a vital necessity in our generation,

Especially in light of the well-known *Iggeres HaKodesh* which states that when his wellsprings spread outward, the Master, King Moshiach, will come.

May this be accomplished in tranquility and with a good heart.

With respect and blessing.

***

As a token of appreciation, I am transcribing here a few notes on your book, even though "one does not offer critiques on a *drush*."

**Opening of the Gate:** Regarding the naming of a child, etc., and that it is from the Arizal: It is in *Shaar HaGilgulim*, Introduction 23. See also *Emek HaMelech*, Gate 1, end of Chapter 4.

**At the beginning, 1:1:** "Rabbi Yitzchak said..." that this refers to the beginning of the holy Torah, etc. It is difficult to say that this is the intent, since Rabbi Yitzchak explains his intent and concludes: "Elokim" (and there is no Elokim but Truth), and "Hashem Elokim is Truth." It should be explained here in the manner of what is written in *Matnos Kehunah* there (*Bereishis Rabbah*, Chapter 1:7). See also *Baal HaTurim*.

**At the end, 246-7:** That Moshe is the totality of all souls and Yehoshua is the totality of all spirits. This is stated in *Ohr HaChaim* (Pinchas 27:18). This requires further study and a search for the source.

**Ibid:** "The end of the Torah is embedded in its beginning," based on what is explained regarding "which Moshe did... before the eyes of all Israel"—the concept of *semichah* (ordination). It can be explained as the connection to "Bereishis"—based on the *Targum Yonasan* (and *Zohar Chadash* 3a) that it means "**in wisdom**," which is the essence of *semichah*. This is further clarified by what Admur the Tzemach Tzedek writes in his *Sefer HaMitzvos*, in the Mitzvah of *Kiddush HaChodesh* (and sources cited there), that the "eye" and "eyes of the congregation" refer to the external aspect of *Chochma* (*Netzach* of *Abba*), and the ordination (*semichah*) must be performed through the internal aspect of *Chochma* (*Yesod* of *Abba*). This is why it adds "Bereishis" after having mentioned just before, "the eyes of all Israel."

## H'Shama

B"H, 25 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letters, which outline your activities in the various areas you have taken upon yourselves, and regarding the primary matter: the education of the holy Torah in Chabad schools.

The common denominator in all of them, at least according to what you write, is that there is a lack of order in your work. Consequently, you find yourselves in a state of ups and downs, etc.

Now, one of the intentions behind everyone being together in a Chabad school is that "one man helps his fellow" and strengthens him in the aforementioned work. In all of these, there must be order, as demanded by our Rebbes, the Nesi'im, as explained in several sichos kodesh. In education, order is essential, especially since we see tangibly that order is vital for the sustainability and success of these activities—and that which is well-known requires no proof.

However, we do not cry over the past. The hope is that, at the very least, from here on in there will be no room for "lowering oneself" [feeling discouraged]. With the necessary seriousness, yet at the same time with joy and a glad heart, you will organize your activities as they should be, and you will succeed, going from strength to strength, increasing in light.

I await good news regarding all that has been mentioned and detailed as required.

## 8342

B"H, 26 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of Tuesday:

1) Regarding the study of our Torah’s teachings, both in the revealed part and in the part of Chassidus: if someone says that it is not having an effect on his daily routine, is it worth continuing?

It is surprising that there is any doubt about such a simple matter. The mitzvah of Talmud Torah is incumbent upon every single Jew—not only the tzaddik and the beinoni, but even those on a lower level, and even further down. On the contrary, we have been promised that even a little bit of the light of Torah pushes away much darkness (the darkness of the kelipos and the sitra achra).

2) Regarding your writing that perhaps these are "delicate matters" (*eidele inyanim*), and one should refrain from studying them with the aforementioned person:

What could be more "delicate" than what is written: "In the beginning, G-d created the heavens and the earth," and similarly, "I am Hashem your G-d," etc.? It is certainly forbidden—and even the doubt itself is misplaced—to question whether to study these verses with any given person. And you understand the implication.

3) Regarding your question on what is written in Tanya, Chapter 29, that there it is "above time," and it is "as if he has blemished and defiled the day," G-d forbid, literally: Seemingly, based on this, what is the practical difference between times that are auspicious and set for teshuvah—according to the instruction of our Torah, the Torah of life—and the rest of the year, since it is "as if he has blemished and defiled the day"?

In general, it is not understood what the connection is, for the times set for teshuvah mean that at those times, teshuvah is accepted more readily and is easier to achieve. But in truth, there is no difficulty regarding the "blemish" either; for it is explained in Tanya there, that "as if he has blemished and defiled" refers to that specific level and world to which his teshuvah has not reached, or which is insufficient for the level he stands on today—which is not the case regarding the level where he has already performed complete teshuvah and it has been accepted.

4) Regarding what is explained in certain places, that thought is effective for another person: is this only for those on a high level, or also regarding every Jew?

One should learn from the statement of Chazal regarding lashon hara, that it "kills three," which is spoken of regarding every Jew. Thought is not far removed from the matter of speech; on the contrary, in several details, it is more effective and impactful.

5) The method of rectification for the known matter in our time is the concept of *hesech hada'as* (diverting one's attention). *Hesech hada'as* does not mean to wage war with the negative thought, but rather to divert one's attention from it and to move the power of thought to occupy itself with another matter. And how good it is to occupy it with the letters of Torah and tefillah; for if a little light pushes away much darkness, then how much more so does a great deal of light.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Sh. M. Simpson
Secretary

P.S. I am sure you know of and keep the three daily study portions of Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya. In any case, you should keep them from now on.

## 8,443

B"H, 28 Shevat, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 25 Shevat, in which you write regarding the state of your livelihood, noting that there are those who are pursuing you, etc.

Often, it seems to a person that someone is pursuing them, even when the matter is not always so in reality, or at the very least, not as acute as it appears to them. Since a person is biased toward himself, you should consult with your friends in your area regarding this. This is especially true since, as you write, the matter is connected to your livelihood, a field in which those who live in your area are better able to offer advice.

It is certainly understood and simple that conducting one’s daily life in accordance with the instructions of our Torah—the Torah of life—and through the fulfillment of its mitzvos, regarding which it is said, "and he shall live by them," draws down the blessing of Hashem in all that is needed. King David of Israel already said, "Hashem is with me; I will not fear; what can man do to me?"

Strengthening one’s trust in Hashem, who watches over each and every person with individual providence, adds to the blessing of Hashem and draws it down more quickly. And this is easy to understand.

It would be appropriate to have your tefillin checked, and also that every weekday, before morning davening—regardless of the pressure regarding your livelihood—you should set aside a few coins for tzedakah.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 844

[30 Shevat, 5722]

Regarding your inquiry about youth whose mother is not Jewish: The law is clear that the status follows the mother. Consequently, it is forbidden to teach them Torah (except for the Seven Mitzvos of the Bnei Noach). Furthermore—and perhaps even more importantly—their inclusion in a Jewish youth organization is highly likely to lead to their assimilation, *chas v’shalom*, into the Jewish community without any formal conversion (even if the matter is occasionally mentioned to them). The explanation—that one should teach them and wait until they are eighteen to address the question of conversion—clearly does not permit the aforementioned prohibition, nor does it **at all** eliminate the aforementioned concern. Moreover, regarding conversion, the determining age is only thirteen.

#### ***844***

Published in *Shaarei Halacha U’Minhag*, Vol. 3, p. 245.

## 850

B"H, Rosh Chodesh Adar I, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Blessings and peace!

In response to your letter, in which you write that you have become engaged to Mr. [Name], may he live, who is a shomer mitzvos and possesses good character traits, and that you have set the wedding date for a few weeks from now. However, you are distressed that he does not practice covering his head and is unwilling to do so now, even though he promises that he will conduct himself this way once he leaves Morocco in the middle of the summer, claiming that covering the head is merely a matter of chassidus.

Since, as you write, he is a man of good character and a shomer mitzvos, he will surely accept the explanation that even if we were to concede that the aforementioned matter is only one of chassidus, when one prepares to marry—that is, to build a "lasting edifice" (*binyan adei-ad*), as the phrasing of the wedding blessings goes—one requires a special blessing from the Giver of the Torah and the Commander of the mitzvos. As we say in many prayers, "To You, Hashem, is the righteousness, and to us is the shame of face," yet despite this, we ask the Ribono shel Olam to bless the couple in every detail.

And since the attribute of the Ribono shel Olam is "measure for measure," as our Sages of blessed memory have said, it is certainly incumbent upon a person who is in need of the aforementioned blessing to conduct himself not only according to what is strictly required by the letter of the law, but to do even that which is only a matter of chassidus. Especially since he says that after a period of time he will cover his head, the entire matter is merely a question of advancing that conduct by a few months—and in exchange for this, they will receive an increase in Hashem's blessing.

Regarding the substance of the matter, that he says covering the head is only a measure of chassidus: the halachic ruling has already been established that the matter of covering the head is obligatory for every shomer Torah u'mitzvos. This is not only according to those poskim who hold that it is required by the law of the Gemara and the Rishonim, but even those who disagree and say otherwise also concede that **in these generations of ours**, the matter of uncovering the head is an indication of casting off the yoke [and that through this, one strives that he should not be recognized as a son of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov, who learns Torah and keeps mitzvos, and that people should not say of him that, Heaven forbid, he is not of the children of Yisrael, for his walk and dress resemble that of a non-Jew]. From this it is understood that not only is one obligated to cover the head by law, but among the laws themselves, this is a specific law that touches upon the aforementioned concept of "You have chosen us from among all the nations," and it is obvious that there is no place to emulate them (the nations) and to conduct oneself in a way that would lead others to mistakenly think that he is not, Heaven forbid, of the children of Yisrael.

Enclosed is a booklet that discusses the matter of covering the head, and you will surely find those in your environment who understand English to explain the content of the booklet to him.

May it be Hashem's will that you bring good news regarding all of the above.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 8th of Adar

B"H, Erev Rosh Chodesh Adar, 5722
Brooklyn.

To the congregants of the Anshei Lubavitch Synagogue in the city of Passaic,
May Hashem protect them and grant them life,

Shalom u’vracha!

I was pleased to receive regards from you through the esteemed Chossid, a man of integrity and distinction, engaged in communal needs, R’ Aharon Moshe, may he live, who also delivered your contribution, for which the receipt is enclosed.

In accordance with the parsha of this week, which begins with the command of Hashem, "And this is the contribution that you shall take from them: gold, etc.," the Midrash (Terumah 35) states: "Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish said: The world was not worthy of using gold. Why, then, was it created? For the sake of the Mishkan and for the sake of the Beis Hamikdash."

"The world is not worthy of using gold; it was only created to be used for the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash."

The hint—my intention in this—is that when one gives gold for matters of Torah and mitzvos, it brings down gold into the world.

May it likewise be for everyone who gives a contribution for matters of tzedakah, that they should receive the blessing of Hashem in that which they give, and in all their affairs in general.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 8,347

B"H, 3 Adar I, 5722
Brooklyn

After a very long interval, I was pleased to receive regards from you through the man of character and energy, Rabbi Shmuel Pesach, may he live and be well, Bogomilsky, as well as your letter.

In other words, that the intellect of the head should be permeated with the study of Torah, the Yiddishe heart should feel for Torah and Yiddishkeit, and one should act in daily life in that spirit and according to the instructions of our Torah, Toras Chaim.

And one sees clearly and distinctly that if one decides to conduct oneself as stated above with true strength, it is much easier than it had seemed previously.

And what is more, one can do this with good health, a good mood, and with simcha.

With blessings for good tidings in all that was written above, and best regards to all the friends and acquaintances in your camp, may they live and be well.

## 848

B"H, 4 Adar, 5722
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and energetic communal worker, etc.,
R' Moshe Yechiel, may he live, and his wife, the Rabbanit Mindel, may she live,

Greetings and blessings!

I am in receipt of your letter from 1 Adar I, as well as the one preceding it.

The practical difference between a mikveh where the water is drawn from above and one where there is an opening in the floor of the mikveh is, as is understood, very great: there is a concern of *zichilah* (especially according to those who hold that the prohibition of *zichilah* is *min haTorah*), even if there is no doubt whatsoever regarding the seal, and especially when the seal is made of a material that is susceptible to *tumah*. It is surprising that the individual you mention is unaware of the above.

I believe you have the sefer *Taharat HaMayim* by R' N. Telushkin, and you will find the above mentioned there as well.

It is understood and simple that one should endeavor to have two mikvaos [and the more, the better].

With blessings for good tidings in communal and personal matters — and in connection with your birthday, for a year of success in revealed goodness, both generally and specifically.

## 839

B"H, 4 Adar, 5722
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and upright man, who engages in communal needs and conducts his business with integrity,
R' Yaakov HaKohen, may he live and be well,

Shalom u'Bracha!

In response to your letter of 2 Adar I, along with the enclosed.

At an auspicious time, I will mention your mother, may she live and be well, at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in accordance with the contents of your letter.

And whatever you decide, may it be for the good, in a way that is visibly and revealedly good.

In general, in matters involving moving from one place to another, especially at such an age, if there is any doubt, it is better to "sit and do nothing" (*shev v'al ta'aseh*).

As we read in this week’s parsha, Parshas Terumah, we begin reading in the Torah about the construction of the Mishkan (and as our Sages of blessed memory say, the Beis HaMikdash is also called a Mishkan). Seemingly, since the matter concerns Hashem, the neshamah, and its avodah, why were all these thirteen or fifteen materials required to set up the Mishkan? One could have studied Torah and davened with great kavanah in a completely ordinary house or courtyard. The same applies to the offering of korbanos, which could have been done on a *bamah*—a place in the middle of a field or in a city, without buildings surrounding it.

However, we see from the parsha that Hashem desires that we give gold, silver, copper, and so on, and from these, create a vessel for Him, may He be blessed, and that we should daven there—for tefillah corresponds to the korbanos—and likewise for the study of Torah, which is the *Kodesh HaKodashim* (where there is nothing more than the Aron, the Luchos, and a Sefer Torah).

From this, there is a lesson for all times: that Hashem, may He be blessed, gives and continues to give Yidden gold, silver, etc., so that they may carry out His inner desire that specifically in the lower worlds there should be a dwelling place for Him, may He be blessed.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 850

B"H, Motzaei Shabbos Kodesh, Parshas V'asu Li Mikdash V'shachanti B'socham, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing and scholarly, whose work is the work of Heaven,
Moreinu HaRav Hillel, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

I was pleased to receive your letter informing me that you have been appointed to serve in holiness, in a rabbinate of pure holiness at the synagogue, a *mikdash me'at*, located at 10 Rue Cadet in the 9th arrondissement of Paris.

May it be the will [of Hashem] that this appointment be in a good and successful hour—and as is well known, the precise meaning of "success" (*hatzlacha*) is something that transcends all estimation.

This means that there should be an increase in all matters of the synagogue—both in matters of *tefillah* and in the *shiurei Torah* studied in public and in private. As is well known, the *drashah* of our Sages of blessed memory on the verse, "Hashem loves the gates of Zion more than all the dwellings of Yaakov," refers to "gates that are distinguished (*metzuyanim*) in *halacha*" (*Berachos 8a*).

This is especially so in light of what I recall from the years when I lived in Paris and would daven at regular times in that aforementioned synagogue; this *mikdash me'at* is capable of fostering both *tefillah* and Torah, so that it may be fitting for its name—a *mikdash me'at*, the house of the King, the King of the kings of the world (*Megillah 27a*, see there).

May it be the will that all of the above be founded upon and permeated with the love of Hashem and the fear of Hashem, which are the root and the sustenance of all the aforementioned, as hinted at in Rambam, *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah*, beginning of chapter [2], and explained at greater length in *Sefer Tanya Kaddisha*, beginning of chapter 4.

We are currently in the weeks during which we read in the Torah the manner in which the command, "And they shall make Me a sanctuary and I will dwell among them," is fulfilled—"among them" (*b'socham*), not "within it" (*b'socho*), but "among them"—within each and every Jew (see on this the *maamar* entitled *HaYosheves B'Ganim*, 5748).

With blessings for success in all the above, and with regards to the congregants, the members, and the *gabbaim* at their head, of the synagogue and its surroundings—may they live and be well.

## 851

851

B"H, 7 Adar, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

... At a time of favor, may you mention all those whom you write about at the holy gravesite.

Regarding what you wrote about the trait of anger — see Tanya, Iggeres HaKodesh, Chapter 2, and Iggeres HaKodesh, Letter 25 — one of the pieces of advice for this is to firmly resolve that whenever you feel anger, you will restrain yourself from speaking for at least a few minutes. It is seen tangibly that through this, the anger dissipates.

Regarding what you wrote about Ahavas Yisroel, etc. — become proficient (at least in the content) in the Maamar in Sefer HaMitzvos of the Tzemach Tzedek on the mitzvah of Ahavas Yisroel. And review this from time to time according to your own personal capacity.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 8,352

B"H, 7 Adar, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from Tuesday, and in response to your question as to whether studying a chapter of Tanya each day is sufficient to fulfill the obligation of studying *pnimiyus haTorah*.

It is surprising that there is any doubt regarding this, for it is a general rule in all the *mitzvos* of the Torah that one must perform them "as one who is required to do it." Certainly, one can study, understand, and intellectually grasp other *seforim* of *Toras HaChassidus*. This is especially true in light of what is explained in the *reshimos* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *Nasi Yisrael* (printed in *Kitzurim VeHe'aros LeSefer HaTanya*), that the *Sefer HaTanya* in relation to other *seforim* of *Chassidus* is like the Written Torah in relation to the Oral Torah. This is based on the points made there, and in the language of the Alter Rebbe in his *Iggeres HaKodesh*, that the matters in the Written Torah—and even its *mitzvos*—are not revealed, known, and explicit except through the Oral Torah (see there, *siman* 29, at length).

It is the same regarding *Toras HaChassidus*, and this is easy to understand.

Regarding your writing about the *seforim* you have in your possession, etc.—you reside among *anash* in the Holy City of Jerusalem, *tizkeh lichon*, and the statement of our Sages regarding the virtue of one who possesses *seforim* and lends them to others is well-known.

Regarding your writing at the end of your letter, that the *Zohar* and the writings of the *Arizal* are also termed *pnimiyus haTorah*, and what is the emphasis that *pnimiyus haTorah* was revealed in *Toras HaChassidus*?

This question is also puzzling, for that which is visibly apparent requires no testimony or explanation. My meaning is: when one compares the understanding of a concept as studied in the *Zohar* to the understanding of it as explained in *Toras HaChassidus*—and similarly regarding the writings of the *Arizal* [for example, one should study a concept explained in *Biurei HaZohar* and before that in the *Zohar* itself, and] it is analogous to someone studying only the *Mishnah* without the *Gemara* and its commentaries. There is no need to elaborate on such a simple matter.

It is my hope that you participated in the *farbrengen* of the *yom hilula* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, the tenth of Shevat, who sacrificed himself for the dissemination of the aforementioned study, even though you do not mention this in your letter.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 853

853

B"H, 8 Adar, 5722
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 2 Adar, in which you outline the main chapters of your life and conclude with the dilemma currently at hand: the matter of employment after your wedding, b’sha’ah tovah umutzlach. You detail the pros and cons of both options.

Since the success of teaching is diminished by the teacher’s fatigue (from commuting, etc.), and it is likely that in [the city of]... aside from the substitute teaching position, other opportunities will soon arise—at the very least as a supplementary source of income (whether in the literal sense, or for spiritual sustenance, or both)—you should prefer the position in...

Regarding the conclusion of your letter, where you mention your assumption that the wedding would specifically take place on a day when it is permitted to fast:

The verse and the explanation of our Sages, of blessed memory, are well known: "They were given by one Shepherd." This means that the matter of fasting on the day of the chuppah, and the instruction that one should not fast on Rosh Chodesh—but rather on the day preceding it, which carries the same weight as fasting on the day of the chuppah—were both given by one Shepherd. They are one and the same. This absolutely refutes the expression in your letter, "which, to my regret, I did not succeed in doing."

As mentioned, may the wedding be b’sha’ah tovah umutzlach, in both the physical and spiritual realms together, so that they may walk hand in hand; for "Israel is one nation on earth," and they draw down this unity into all their affairs.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 8554

B"H, 8 Adar I, 5722
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from the 2nd of Adar I, in which you write that you have met with a bochur several times and have decided that he is suitable for you, etc., and you ask for my opinion on this matter.

From the tone of your letter, it is evident that both of you have decided to establish your home upon the foundations of Torah and mitzvos, a traditional Jewish home—both the bochur and yourself.

Based on this, the proposal is appropriate, and may it be in a good and auspicious hour.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Sh. M. Simpson
Secretary

**P.S.** The surprise expressed in your letter is understandable, given that the bochur is a ben yeshiva but does not have a beard, and works in the production of tefillin.

It is well known how highly our Sages of blessed memory—and especially the holy Zohar—spoke of the matter of growing a beard. This is especially true when the person in question is a ben yeshiva who is engaged in the production of tefillin—that is, a task of the utmost holiness. As our Sages of blessed memory expressed it, the entire Torah is compared to tefillin, and the Holy Name, the Honored Name, is mentioned in them twenty-one times. All those who wear these tefillin over time are dependent upon the yiras shamayim of the one who is engaged and working in the production of the tefillin.

May it be Hashem’s will that, at the very least, from this point forward, the aforementioned avreich will grow his beard. And according to what is explained in the holy Zohar, through this, the blessing of the Ribono shel Olam will also increase in all that is needed.
